Date: Mon, 01 Jun 92 09:10 EDT
From: "Bruce.Pigozzi" <22107BWP@msu.edu>
Subject: Gas

Those who recalled the Consumers Report article on gas were right.  I tracked
down the article and its subsequent updates and corrections (last one was 6/91
I think).  Basically, relying on company self reporting, the following brands
have fuels with additives which pass the "BMW unlimited mileage" test in
regular, mid, and premium grades: Amoco, Atlantic, Chevron,
Diamond-Shamrock,Exxon, Fina, Gulf(Cumberland Farms), Hess, Kerr-McGee,
Marathom, Mobil, Phillips 66, Shell, Sunoco, Texaco. Conoco regular & premium.

Those whose premium grade only passes the BMW unlimited mileage test
are:Citgo, Clark, Getty, and BP west of Johnstown PA.  As I understand it BP
east of Johnstown is different and doesn't pass at any level.  Arco seemed
confused. There is a table in the April 91 issue of CR but a correction/update
adds a few more later on, none are deleted.  It would seem therefore, that if
the owners manual says regular fuel is fine for your Porsche you need only
stay with a premium BRAND to get the additives which will reduce/eliminate
fuel based deposits on valves.

Subject: Porsche gears up for women drivers.
Date: Mon, 1 Jun 1992 14:50:56 +0100

From:	NAME: Philip Katz           [DIP11 ]
	FUNC: DIP11                           
	TEL: 0394-693446                      <KATZ P M AT WEB AT WEBCS>
To:	porschephiles@karazm.math.uh.edu@UNET


	From London's _Evening Standard_ of 28-MAY-1992, written by Ian
Morton and reproduced without comment :


	Forget wind in the hair macho motorists, women drivers are the next
target for the sports car.

	Porsche, which supplied many of the wheels for the upwardly mobile
in the Eighties and nosedived into the recession with them, is set
for a comeback. But this time, "new woman" is the target.

	Porsche's new 968 will trigger a new marketing approach, says UK
sales manager John Edwards. Women executives are "a growing and
significant force in business and will be specifically targeted -
this is not sexist, it is an accurate way to address our market".
Porsche's 968 replaces the 944 beloved of the yuppies and teenage
scribblers who helped the firm to a record 3,700 sales in the UK
four years ago. And Porsche makes no apologies for its rather
similar appearance - though 82 per cent of components are new or
modified, the 968 is an evolution of the previous model.

	Major differences include an engine that automatically adjusts its
timing and power output to demand. The price of progress looks steep
- the manual fixed-head version will cost #34,945, nearly #6,000
more than the replaced model, and the cabrio #38,724, an increase of
more than #8,000.

	"The 968 will reach 60mph in 6.5 seconds - but will stop safely
from 60 in less than three seconds," said Edwards.

I beliver the reason your high performance Porsche has an
85 MPH speedometer is that it was the law back then. The theory
by the insurance companies, et al, was that if you could not see
numbers greater than 85 mph you would never want to driver faster than
that and the world would be safe. That law was rescinded around 1984-85.

Subject: Solutions to 944 electrical problems.
Date: Tue, 2 Jun 1992 09:42:16 +0100

From:	NAME: Philip Katz           [DIP11 ]
	FUNC: DIP11                           
	TEL: 0394-693446                      <KATZ P M AT WEB AT WEBCS>
To:	porschephiles@karazm.math.uh.edu@UNET


	When I acquired my 85.5 944, there were a number of niggly
electrical problems with it, many of which I managed to fix with the
assistance of people on this list. In the hope that they may be of use to
others, I've summarised the problems and solutions ... e-mail me if you
want more details on any of these. There are also a couple of problems
which I've not yet fixed - any help on these would be appreciated.

	Car : 1985 ('86 Model Year, i.e. teardrop dash) 944 Lux, 2.5
litre, UK spec, 53k miles, manual transmission. Stock.

PROBLEM : One of the twin horns wasn't working.
SOLUTION : The gasket on the horn had leaked, allowing the innards to
corrode. I tried dismantling, cleaning and remantling (is there such a
word?) but to no avail and ended up having to buy a new horn :-(

PROBLEM : The windscreen washer did not work in the "only spray water but
don't activate the wipers" position.
SOLUTION : I suspect that the previous owners had never used this position
on the switch (you pull it back one position to just spray the screen and
further back to activate the wipers as well). I removed the switch (after
taking off the steering wheel - and despite claims to cover this model,
the Haynes manual does *not* tell you how) and sprayed contact cleaner
into it. After repeating this treatment, it all works fine.

PROBLEM : The MPG gauge stopped working - it went to maximum (0 mpg) when
the ignition was on and stayed there.
SOLUTION : This took me the longest to trace, about 12 hours in total.
There is a red plastic connector in the passenger compartment and one of
the pins had come adrift, possibly when my alarm was installed. I only
discovered it when I was at the "lets take *everything* apart and spray it
with contact cleaner" stage. Kevin Gross kindly snail-mailed me a wiring
diagram which helped me identify it.

PROBLEM : The windscreen washer was fine on the driver's side but only
spluttered on the passenger side.
SOLUTION : There is a one-way valve in the plumbing to each of the washer
jets which had seized on the passenger side - this took me by surprise as
I know little about plumbing and never expected a valve there!

PROBLEM : The tilting roof intermittently doesn't.  
SOLUTION : I wish I knew - this one's still not resolved. I've tried
dismantling the motor mechanism and cleaning it as best I can. The switch
and relay seem OK as when the roof doesn't open there is still a whirring
sound from the motor.  The problem is intermittent - sometimes the roof
won't open at all, other times it partly opens and yet other times it all
works fine. It usually will close OK.  Could this have anything to do with
the micro switches near the motor, or worn brushes?

PROBLEM : The speedometer intermittently doesn't work at speeds below about
30mph.
SOLUTION : This is the other one I haven't figured out. Sometimes the
speedometer stays at rest until I'm doing about 25-30mph, then it works
fine. Again this is intermittent. I've had a quick look at the leads going
to the speedo sender and they seem OK - could the sender be duff? Is it
expensive to replace?

>> From: katz_p_m@bt-web.bt.co.uk

>> PROBLEM : The tilting roof intermittently doesn't.  
>> SOLUTION : I wish I knew - this one's still not resolved...

I had a problem similar to this on my 1988 944. It turned out to be
the two plastic gears which lift the two supports at the rear of the
sunroof. The motor turns a worm gear cable which turns one end of the
plastic gears. The other part of the plastic gear raises the roof.
In my case the roof stuck the first time I raised the roof in the
spring and caused the motor to strip the plastic gears. Needless to
say, I now push the roof up manually once when it has not been
opened for a long time prior to using the motor. Hope this helps.

Peter Tracy
pgt@keps.kodak.com

My '85 944 had a problem with the sunroof also. Sometimes when I went to "open"
it and the last thing I had done with it was "tilt", nothing would happen. If I
used the tilt again it would then start to work. I always put this down to being
slightly out of adjustment. It was too intermittent to bother with. I think
yours is a similar problem to mine. Does the problem always occur when you last
used the roof open? Another think I noticed was about 30sec after the ignition
was turned on, the sunroof motor would turn on for an instant as if
retightening itself. I always thought it must take a german mind to have a
timer recheck the roof after starting the car ;-)

|PROBLEM : The speedometer intermittently doesn't work at speeds below about
|30mph.
|fine. Again this is intermittent. I've had a quick look at the leads going
|to the speedo sender and they seem OK - could the sender be duff? Is it
|expensive to replace?
|
The speedometer is an easy problem to diagnose. The jump up to the current speed
from 0 when you reach 25-30 is because the guage itself is sticking. only when
the force is enough will it pull away from the point at which it sticks. I have
not taken a gauge out of a Porsche so I do not know whether it is fixable, nor
do I know how much a new guage would be. I guess you could always try fixing it
and if all else fails then replace.

From: "Kevin C. Gross" <0004056890@mcimail.com>

Subject: Re: Solutions to 944 electrical problems.

Philip -

>PROBLEM : The tilting roof intermittently doesn't.  
>SOLUTION : I wish I knew - this one's still not resolved. I've tried
>dismantling the motor mechanism and cleaning it as best I can. The switch
>and relay seem OK as when the roof doesn't open there is still a whirring
>sound from the motor.  The problem is intermittent - sometimes the roof
>won't open at all, other times it partly opens and yet other times it all
>works fine. It usually will close OK.  Could this have anything to do with
>the micro switches near the motor, or worn brushes?

There is a fair-sized collection of electrical and mechanical stuff which runs
the roof.  There's a detailed trouble-shooting guide in a tech bulletin and in
the workshop manuals.  There have also been a series of tech bulletins
describing lifting roof repair do's and don'ts.

The micro-switches are certainly worth checking.  I've heard them blamed before.
I would myself get the wiring diagram out and go after the system with a
multi-meter, figure out how everything works and what doesn't.

Kevin

This list is still being run as a mail reflector, which sends incoming
mail to the whole list at once. The participants include people with a 
variety of backgrounds, some technical, some race/autocross/performance 
drivers, some owners, some just admirers.  

To send a message to the whole list, mail to

		porschephiles@karazm.math.uh.edu

To send mail regarding distribution problems, or other administrative issues,
send mail to

		porschephiles-request@karazm.math.uh.edu


The list is also available as a digest, where you get a compilataion of
the day's messages once per day, rather than getting all of the individual
messages. This seems to be a win for people with slow mail links or who
are just too busy to stay current. If you want to convert to (or from)
the digest, send mail to the porschephiles-request address above.

PLEASE NOTE A FEW ITEMS:

1) There are a lot of  members on the list. It's small enough to be effective,
   and large enough to have a number of diverse opinions and sets of
   experience. Remember that before you decide to not ask a question, or
   if you get flamed for asking an "obvious" question. Diversity -- it's
   a *feature*....

2) The list is maintained irregularly. If I'm in town or can reach the
   host system from wherever I am and I have the time to deal with it,
   I *try* to handle changes on a real-time basis. However, as a consultant
   I travel extensively and from time to time, changes will be batched
   on a weekly basis.

3) Please try to send all administrative mail to the "porschephiles-request"
   address, or to me directly. And, as some members have discovered,
   you can also reach me by phone (sometimes).

4) In the same vein, if you use a "pure" System V mail system, please pay
   attention to your replies. The default will send the reply to 
   the "porschephiles-request" address, as it is the UUCP "From "
   address (first actual line of the mail header). 

   PLEASE BE AWARE THAT I TRASH SUCH MIS-ROUTED MAIL!!!

5) If your mailer starts screwing up *you will be dropped from the list*
   Should you notice that you aren't getting the list any more, send me
   mail and I'll figure out why not...

Enjoy the list! If I can be of assistance, please don't hesitate to drop
me a note.

  (5.65c/IDA-1.5 for <porschephiles@math.UH.EDU>); Wed, 3 Jun 1992 07:40:50 -0500
Message-Id: <199206031240.AA27016@math.UH.EDU>
From: fwk@homxc.att.com
Date: Wed, 3 Jun 92 08:36 EDT
Original-From: homxc!fwk (F W Kerfoot, Iii +1 908 949 6348)
To: porschephiles@math.UH.EDU
Subject: Re: 914 warm air guides - needed?

(Mark Skala writes:)
>On the '73 914 I recently bought, I noticed that the 2 'warm air guides'
>which are normally mounted under the engine about where the pushrod tubes
>go, are not there.  Can anyone tell me for sure whether 
> (a) these are strictly heater system parts
> (b) these are guides to help the fan cool the engine
> (c) both (a) and (b)
>
The sheet metal sections I assume you are talking about simply guide a
small part of the cooling air delivered by the engine fan to the heat
exchangers, where that air is heated, and then delivered (through the
"J tubes" and the flexible air hoses) to channels within the car's
chassis and thus to the interior, for heat (and to the dashboard area,
for defrost).

To answer your question specifically, I believe that you can run the car
without those ducts in place without fear of overheating the engine.
The only danger would be that the presence of the ducts and heat exchangers
would restrict the air flow enough that when they are absent, too much
of the cooling fan air is lost through the disconnected openings. I believe
this is a very small risk. If you are concerned, you could block the
unused cooling fan output openings with duct tape or the like.

Frank Kerfoot
fwk@homxc.att.com

I know what parts you are refering to, but those are not what I am worrying
about.  The 'warm air guides' (I've also heard them called 'lower deflector
plate' right and left) are roughly trapezoidal pieces of sheet metal which
cover a section of the bottom of the engine.  I believe it was Kevin Gross
who already responded that these parts are necessary for proper engine
cooling, but I would like to hear any experience to the contrary.  I'm
going to get these parts and install them, but who knows how long it's
been driven without them so far.

Two questions for the group:

1) What do you get when you put a large (5 year-old) Mercedes engine in a small
BMW?  (Hint: yes, this is a Porsche question.)

2) Porsche supposedly changed the model number of the 911 from 901 because
Peugeot claimed that all three-digit model numbers with a middle zero were
reserved for them. How many European (non-Peugeot) models of the 50's and
60's had such numbers? (You can restrict to those where the number was not
keyed to engine displacement, like Mercedes 200 and 300; maybe they could
get a waiver from Peugeot.)




























































1) A 356 (cf. 320, 325, and Merc. 560).

2) Incomplete list: BMW 502, 503(?), 507; Bristol 401, 402, 403, 404, 405
(note 401 and 403 are Peugeot model numbers); Facel Vega HK500. Also involving
capacity: BMW 700, Ferrari Dino 206.

From: edwards@jargon.whoi.edu (Gary Edwards)
Message-Id: <9206031443.AA01044@jargon.whoi.edu>
To: vwp@cbnewsk.att.com
Subject: Re: 914 warm air guides - needed?
Cc: porschephiles@karazm.math.uh.edu

Mark,

The trapeziodal(sp?) warm air guides on the bottom of the 914 engine are positively not required for engine cooling.  Additionally, they do not use or 
block any other cooling fan air outlets.  Their deletion may reduce the 
effectiveness of your heating system and the lack of a left side one may affect
the calibration of the thermostat for the movable cooling flaps since it will
now be operating in cooler air.  I suspect their absence actually improves 
cooling once the thermostat opens the air flappers all the way.

When I bought my first 914, I had the same concern as you and actually ordered
new ones.  Now I seldom even put them on the cars.

Gary Edwards

73 914 2.0
73 914 2.0
73 914 2.0
74 914 2.0

Phillip and Kevin write:


>2. The garage which did my last service has gone out of business :-( and
>it's time for a 6k service, so I'm now trying another one which was
>recommended by people in the local Porsche Club. I had planned to go for
>Mobil 1 oil after reading good things about it here, but this garage only
>uses Shell oils. They tell me that they use 15/50 normally for 944s, and
>only advise using Gemini in turbos. 

>a. Is Gemini as good as Mobil 1? Are all synthetic oils much the same?
>[Hope I don't start any religious wars!]

>b. Should I go for Gemini rather than 15/50, or should I bring my own
>Mobil 1? The 15/50 is 2 pounds per litre and the Gemini 3.60 pounds, so it
>wouldn't add an awful lot to the service costs.

>       The car is an 85.5 UK spec 944 Lux, 53k miles, absolutely stock,
>used only for road use.

I have never heard of Gemini.  I assume it's a UK brand.  Mobil 1 is fine.  In
fact, a street-drive car is just as well off (and your wallet more so) with a
good brand of mineral oil.  I use Castrol 20W50 or 10W40 for no reason other
than its availability at a good price at a local mall.

*******

A friend at work posted (paper on a bulletin board, not electronically) 
a fairly scary note regarding Castrol.  Appearantly (in wet sump engines)
the oil will foam, which supposedly reduces the lubricating properties.

There was also a story about bad smoke problems that were cured by 
switching to another (non-castrol) oil.

Since the 944's are wet sump (I think), I thought (i) this might be 
informative, and (ii) has anyone else hear anything similar?

I can get Kendall for about $1.59/qt, so that's what I've been using.

I suspect that the most important quality of the oil is that it is FRESH.
I change mine about every 3500 miles.  (I shoot for 3k, but...)

I'm **really** glad that Mountain View (where I live) has a curbside
recycling program that picks up motor oil; 911's hold a *lot* of oil.


--Soren            laforce@xenon.arc.nasa.gov

Since we're on the subject of Castrol's problems...  Has anyone heard
about Castrol and the Honda Interceptor motorcycle engines?  I seem
to remember something about Castrol oils lacking zinc disulfide (?).
The Interceptor engine has really tight valve springs, or something.
Zinc disulfide protects alloys similar to cams and lifters.  Owners
who used Castrol (specifically) had to replace their camshafts much
sooner than owners using other oils. "Sooner" meant 1/10 the life of
cams under other oils.  I don't remember this topic very well, but it
was discussed at length in the rec.motorcycles(.racing ?) group a
couple of years ago.  

Can anyone enlighten me on the realities involved here?

Subject: Re:  Redline tran. oil

In this months issue of Road and Track, the technical tidbits section, they 
discussed the use of synthetic oil in the tranny. The editor of the column
stated that syncros need a certain amount of friction to work well. If the 
synthetic is >too< good, then you get the gnashing of teeth and other bad 
things. He also said that is >does< depend on the tranny. Some work remarkably
well with synthetics and some, well, clench thier teeth.  =)
Just a possible suggestion...

--gordonp

Robert,

I too have noticed this problem with 914 alternators.  In fact, every high 
milage 914 alternator I've had out recently has had this problem.  I normally
just take them to the local alternator rebuilder, who replaces the bearings
and checks them out for me for about $45.  I also have friends who do it them-
selves.  I would be uncomfortable with reusing the alternator if it was making
bearing noises.

Jeffrey,

I just did the modification to allow my foglights to come on independently of 
the headlight switch.  It is very well laid out in an article by Allen Caldwell
in one of the volumes of Up-Fixin der Porsche.  I don't have the index with me
here at work, but I think it was one of the later volumes.  By the way, Allen
Caldwell recently put out an index to 914 technical articles which has only
been published in the Connecticutt Valley Region newsletter to the best of my
knowledge.  I'm sure he would provide you one if you wrote him, however.

The modification is easy, and requires only a minor rewiring of the relay and 
switch connections.  If you don't have access to Up-Fixing der Porsche, drop
me a message and I'll see that you get a copy of the article.

Gary

I had the misfortune of having alternator problems on my 911 which I
fixed by purchasing a freshly rebuilt one.  My car had about 130K miles
on it.  If the alternator's old and it makes noise, in my opinion, you'd
be best served by taking it in for a rebuild at your local generator shop.
If you don't, you stand the chance of installing the old one, just to have
it fail a short while later IMHO.

drew

drew_spencer@npd.novell.com
'82 911SC Coupe

Sean writes:

Hi everyone.

A while ago somebody mentioned using Redline in their tranny
with good results.  I'd like to know if anyone else has used
this product and what they can tell us.  My 915 tranny is 
getting very tricky.  when the car(transmission)  gets warm
going to reverse or first is hell.  It grinds pretty bad.  I 
have to hold the clutch for a while.  Could it be a clutch
adjustment?  When the car has some speed (5mph) I can go to 
first gear with no problem.  The grinding noise is only there
when shifting in full stop.

Please help.

***************************************************************

Sean,

I recently replaced whatever oil was in my 915 (transmission) 
with SWEPCO ($50/gal).  Didn't make a bit of difference. :-(

I have to be careful going into first and reverse also.  I usually 
put it into second before going into first or reverse.  Otherwise,
I need to let it sit a few seconds (5-10) with the clutch in before 
putting it into those gears.

I also have to be careful on the first to second transition, strangely
enough, I don't have to be as careful on the third to second shift.
When I described these symptoms to one of the other local 911 owners, 
he suggested that it might be a clutch "hot spot," i.e. clutch not
disengauging completely.  At this point, I think it's just worn 
syncros.

However, I did replace the clutch cable.  Until this time, EVERY time
I have replaced a clutch cable (cars or motorcycles) there has been
a large improvement in the clutch action.  Nada.  Didn't make a bit 
of difference.  This could still help you, I would remove the cable
and check the action, clean, lube (or replace).  Oh yeah, I did
find it necessary to lubricate the new cable (unusual).

If you try the redline, please post your results.


--Soren            laforce@xenon.arc.nasa.gov

I have the same problem on an 83 911SC.  It is worn synchros by
everyones account I have talked to.  I too have to try reverse, or
another gear to get it to go into first sometimes.  This stops one of
the shafts which allows it to go into first more easily.  This is a
chronic problem on 911 trannys.  You CAN NOT force the shift, you just
have to wait for the synchros to do their thing and when the are worn
it gets very bad, especially into first and the first to second shift.

My 911 has 202000 miles on it and I've used swepco in the trans and I
have abused the first to second shift doing stop lite racing against
Detroit iron.  At times it takes 30 seconds of fooling around to get
it into first.

Just one of the things to love/hate about 911's :-).

Subject: Shifting


It seems this gets brought up at least every other month (i'm not
suggesting a FAQ or anything)...

Sythetic Oil in 911 transmissions is fine - it will make shifting a
little harder but will generally preserve the components better since
it "clings" to the metal more than normal oil.  

Everyone should be shifting into first before they stop, double-clutch
into first and then sit at the light/sign with your foot on the
clutch.  The pressure-plate and throw-out bearing, which this
technique will reduce the life of, is far far easier to replace than
the synchros, which this technique will increase the life of.  

=====

Christopher Welty                   Asst. Director, RPI CS Labs, Troy, NY 12180
weltyc@cs.rpi.edu                  "Porsche:  Fahren in seiner schoensten Form"

Subject: 914 warm air guides


 _All_ the sheet metal on the 914 motors is essential to 
 proper cooling. You leave it off at your motors risk.
 This info, I have from a couple of successful 914 racers
 and my shop.

 Additionally, the right-side one (wear the cables run)
 is very useful in protecting your speedometer cable from
 being melted through by heat from the motor. I replaced
 a speedo cable learning this.

 Generally, I would leave parts on Porsches rather than
 leave them off. Most often, they are there for a very good
 reason.

 pedantically, Stef :->

Jeff -

The Upfixin's are published by the Porsche Club of America (PCA), not the
Porsche Owners' Club.  They are collections of all the technical articles which
appear in Panorama, the monthly magazine.  They get published whenever there are
enough articles to merit a new volume.

Michael Todd wrote:
> 2) Porsche supposedly changed the model number of the 911 from 901 because
> Peugeot claimed that all three-digit model numbers with a middle zero were
> reserved for them. How many European (non-Peugeot) models of the 50's and
> 60's had such numbers? 

> 2) Incomplete list: BMW 502, 503(?), 507; Bristol 401, 402, 403, 404, 405

Peugeot also had a 404, which I believe came out in the mid '60s...

Anyway, the point was that Peugeot had these numbers reserved IN FRANCE.  
They could not have done anything in about Porsche selling 901s elsewhere
in Europe, but Porsche did not want to give up (or use a different model
number in) the French market.  I doubt Bristol ever sold their cars in
France -- don't know about BMW's 500-series models.

Drew writes:


I've had the same tranny problems on my '82 911.  I autocross the car
just about every weekend, so the more I had to shift, the worse I got
beat.  The solution.  Double-clutch.  It REALLY works.  I practiced
it every time I drove the car.  It was cumbersome at first, but after
I got the hang of it, it became second nature.  The only problem is that
people look at me funny when I bring the revvs up like I'm hot-rodding or
something.  Or, maybe it's just me they're giving the funny looks? hmmmm.

*************************************************************************

I routinely double clutch every car I drive, every time I downshift.

When I started autocrossing, a new dimension got added:  Double clutching
AND heel-toe'ing.

I had previously put in braided steel brake lines and carefully flushed 
and bled the brakes.  This provided a nice hard brake pedal, but it was 
no where near the same height as the throttle pedal.  I had to shim the 
throttle pedal about 3/4" to get the pedal height (between brake and 
throttle) correct to allow me to heel-toe easily.

If anyone wants to try this, I'd recommend taping (duct tape) a 
piece (or a few pieces) of wood to the throttle pedal to determine just 
how high you need it.  Once I determined the correct height, I cut a new 
wooden shim and attached it with sheet metal screws.



--Soren            laforce@xenon.arc.nasa.gov

>no where near the same height as the throttle pedal.  I had to shim the 
>throttle pedal about 3/4" to get the pedal height (between brake and 
>throttle) correct to allow me to heel-toe easily.

Another solution to this (at least on an older '77 911S) is to adjust the
height of the brake pedal. The linkage has a section that has right hand
threads on one side and left hand threads on the other and a turnbuckle.
This worked quite nicely and never caused any problems.

Mark

Soren writes...

----- Begin Included Message -----

I routinely double clutch every car I drive, every time I downshift.

When I started autocrossing, a new dimension got added:  Double clutching
AND heel-toe'ing.

I had previously put in braided steel brake lines and carefully flushed 
and bled the brakes.  This provided a nice hard brake pedal, but it was 
no where near the same height as the throttle pedal.  I had to shim the 
throttle pedal about 3/4" to get the pedal height (between brake and 
throttle) correct to allow me to heel-toe easily.

If anyone wants to try this, I'd recommend taping (duct tape) a 
piece (or a few pieces) of wood to the throttle pedal to determine just 
how high you need it.  Once I determined the correct height, I cut a new 
wooden shim and attached it with sheet metal screws.



--Soren            laforce@xenon.arc.nasa.gov


----- End Included Message -----


You must have been reading my mind!  I've had the same problem with my
brake pedal after putting on new pads recently.  I'll try the block of
wood to get the height figured out.  Thanks.

Since we're on the subject of heel-toe, what technique do you use?
In the book Driving on Competition by Alan Johnson, he shows his
technique of placing the heel of his foot on the floorboard and rolling
the right side of the ball of his foot onto the throttle to blip.  This 
works great on my new MX-6 as the pedals match perfectly.  On the 911,
I encounter the above-mentioned problems.  I've had others tell me that I
should use the heel on the brake pedal, using the toe to blip.  How do
you people do it???

Drew

Subject: Re:  heel-toe double clutching

In article <9206051635.AA07898@ithaca.Eng.Sandy.Novell.COM> drew_spencer@npd.NOvell.COM (Drew Spencer) writes:
> Since we're on the subject of heel-toe, what technique do you use?
> In the book Driving on Competition by Alan Johnson, he shows his
> technique of placing the heel of his foot on the floorboard and rolling
> the right side of the ball of his foot onto the throttle to blip.  This 
> works great on my new MX-6 as the pedals match perfectly.  On the 911,
> I encounter the above-mentioned problems.  I've had others tell me that I
> should use the heel on the brake pedal, using the toe to blip.  How do
> you people do it???

The technique of heel-and-toe is the same on all cars, but you'll often have
to maneuver your feet differently depending on pedal and footbox
configuration.  I've never driven a 911, but in the 914, I use the ball of
my foot on the brake and the right side and heel on the throttle.  In my
GTI, I use a similar setup, but I've widened the throttle pedal to
facilitate this.  Without it, I use the toes/ball on the brake and the heel
on the throttle.  In other cars, it's heel on the brake, ball on the
throttle.

You'll just have to try it a few different ways to find out what works
best.  Note that if you have particularly wide, narrow, long, or short feet,
what works for someone else might not work for you.  I have a 7.5 AA men's
foot -- shorter than average and *very* narrow.  People with particularly
wide feet (you know, 14 EE) might have trouble hitting only one pedal
sometimes, while I occasionally find my foot slipping between the two.
Heel-and-toe is a very personal subject...
-- 
Mark Sirota - Department of Electrical Engineering Systems Staff
University of Rochester, Rochester NY
 Internet: msirota@ee.rochester.edu

to mash the gas pedal, down near the floor. Works just as well on Type I
VW's.

Subject: Re:  heel-toe double clutching

Drew Spencer writes:

>Ooof! The 911 has the pedal arrangement that ORIGINATED the term "heel and toe"
>what you do is put the toe end of your foot on the brake pedal and use the heel
>to mash the gas pedal, down near the floor. Works just as well on Type I
>VW's

I thought I was the only person in the world who used this method on
Type I's!  It really helped to rev match the old bug given how far
apart the gear ratios are.

In my 84 GTI I use the "Heel-on-the-brake" method.  

When I went to a 1/2 day Jim Russell Driving School "teaser" at Laguna
Seca they were insistent that we use the "Big Toe on the right edge of
the brake, right edge of the foot on the throttle" method.  
This was fine given the extremely tight pedal set up on a FF, but my
size 11.5's had trouble.  I could practically operate all three pedals
with my right foot!

Also, since I have always driven synchro cars, my heel and toe-ing
efforts have always been for fun, not necessity.  As a result, I would use
the rev matching technique simply so that when the clutch re-engaged
in the new gear the revs were already correct as opposed to the engine 
suddenly having to adjust to the new gear ratio...  In the Formula Fords,
of course there was no synchros and the technique was necessary  to change
gears--the shifter *would not* move unless the revs were perfect--well
maybe you could deselect the higher gear but there is no way you could 
engage the lower gear.  After the gear was engaged, the guys at Jim Russell
said to just dump the clutch and not worry about maching the revs to the
new ratio...  probably they knew there was only so much we could handle at
one time...  now that I think of it, they had us heel and toe-ing but not
double clutching.  My guess is that in the full 3 day course they would
have had us double clutching as well.  In a 1/2 day teaser course we
were lucky if we could keep from blowing engines and spinning off the
track to say nothing of double clutch heel-and-toe-ing

I'd hate to see the bottom of the gear box of the Formula Ford I drove
that day... :^)

Vol

>
>> On the 911, I encounter the above-mentioned problems.  I've had others tell 
>> me that I should use the heel on the brake pedal, using the toe to blip.
>> How do you people do it???
>
>Ooof! The 911 has the pedal arrangement that ORIGINATED the term "heel and toe",
>what you do is put the toe end of your foot on the brake pedal and use the heel
>to mash the gas pedal, down near the floor. Works just as well on Type I
>VW's.

On my early 911 (67), I found that the "side-by-side" technique where
the left half of the right foot works the brake pedal and the foot rolls
to the right for the "blip", with the right side of the toe pushing the
gas, worked quite well. That is for size 11C shoes, and, as Mark mentioned,
different techniques may be required for other size feet. Also, I believe
that the pedal details have changed in 911's over the years, which may
also force the use of different techniques. By the way, the same technique
worked fine in my 914, and works fine in my current 944S, after some
tinkering with the pedals.

On the subject of different techniques, I wonder about the alternative
of using the heel on the brake and the toe on the gas. I haven't spent
any time working with this technique, but it seems that it would be
inherently harder to effectively modulate the brakes at the adhesion
limit with the heel, as compared to the toe. Are there any accomplished
race drivers out there, or does anyone know of any, who brake with
their heels? Perhaps I am putting too much emphasis on the racing/track
driving aspects of Porsche driving- I suspect that the either technique
might be fine on the street (unless you need to stop quickly -:) ).

Frank Kerfoot
fwk@homxc.att.com

My two-bits worth.  It is my understanding that both the brake and clutch pedal
heights in 944-series cars can be adjusted.  I think the same is true in the
Carrera's; MFTM adjusted a client's C4 a few weeks ago before she went to her
first driver's ed event.

I haven't changed mine largely because I cannot make up my mind.  At the track,
the brake pedal tends to be buried a lot deeper when I want to heel-toe,
compared to street driving.

I have size 8.5 feet.  I originally tried heel-toeing with the right edge of my
foot on the accelerator, ball on the brake.  Didn't work - getting you foot
stuck between the pedals at the end of a straight is, uh, exciting.  So I have
switched to using the top of my foot on the brake, rotating the heel over to the
gas.  I put my foot higher up on the brake for this than I would otherwise, to
get my heel high enough up on the accelerator so that I have fine enough
leverage.

I have a set of OMP pedal plates I plan to install soon.  The big hope is that
with the much wider accelerator pedal plate, I can go back to the easier,
original technique.

Kevin

> Does anyone have experience repairing alternators that seem fine
> electrically but are mechanically suspect?

As promised, here is a note on how to rebuild a 914 alternator.

Sorry about the line length, but I'm too lazy to fix it...
--------

Alternator Alternative
by Mark Gohlke
Maverick Region

lt seems a shame to me to replace an alternator just because it does  not work any more.  lt 
is even worse to replace one when it is still working, but needs something like bearings 
replaced.  This article specifically addresses my experiences with two 1970-74 914 
alternators, but the comments will generally apply to other models as well.
One of my alternators was suffering from the effects of exposure to the elements and needed 
it's bearings replaced.  The other was old and had apparently been apart before.  It needed 
itUs bearings and  brushes replaced, and was missing a spring washer which belongs behind 
one of the bearings (footnote 1.).  The parts to repair the alternators were surprisingly easy 
to come by, and cost MUCH less than two replacement alternators (rebuilt $130).  Almost all 
bearings are made in standard sizes by bearing manufacturers.  Companies like Bosch who 
make alternators, etc, purchase these bearings to use in their products.  Thus, whenever any 
bearing needs to be replaced - whether in an alternator or somewhere else - the chances are 
very good that the bearing is available at your friendly local bearing distributor.  The same 
goes for oil seals.
These are the parts I found to replace: - Front bearing, part number 6203-z.  $9.00.  The -z  
refers to the seals on the bearing.  They come open, shielded, or sealed on one or both sides.  
The manufacturer's name will also appear, but is not important.  The bearings in my two 
alternators were from Peer-Romania, Fafnir, and Delco -- Pear bearing, part number 6201-
z.  $7.00. -- Brush assembly, part number 021-903-503-A.  $23.00 from your VW/Porsche 
parts man.  This part contains the two brushes and guides mounted to a plate which  also has 
the terminals which plug into the car's wire harness. -- Washer  kit, part  number 02 1 903 
220 $6.00 from your VW/Porsche parts man.  I needed this to get the large spring (wavy) 
washer which was missing from alternator number two.  The kit also contained a spacer, 
some flat washers, and two screws.  None of these parts should really have to be replaced 
unless they are missing.  No doubt the rectifiers can be replaced, but l won't know as long as 
luck is with me.
To disassemble the alternator, first mark the orientation of the two halves and the back 
cover.  The pulley has to be removed to get the front bearing off, so it may as well be done 
first.  The pulley has some slots in it to enable you to hold it to get the nut off.  It will then 
come off easily with a puller.  Remove the back cover, and then remove the long screws 
holding the alternator housing together.  Now the two halves will come apart, although they 
may take some careful prying.  The wide iron ring which divides the two housing halves is the 
stator and must stay with the back (large) half (it has wires connected to it).  The rotor will 
stay with the front half.  Now the bearings and other parts can be removed and replaced.  
Note the bearings are a light press fit onto the rotor.  Use of the proper tools is helpful here.  
Your friendly mechanic, machine shop, or some parts stores can help if necessary at this 
point.
I found that the wires connected to the commutator rings on both rotors were physically 
separated from the rotor.  They had obviously been glued down when they were made, but 
presumably centrifugal force had separated them.  Reposition and rebond the wires if 
necessary.  You may have noticed the ominous sound of the brushes springing from their 
guides as you separated the housing, and sure enough they appear to be blocking all hope of 
ever getting the alternator back together again.  They have to be held out of the way for the 
back bearing to get past them to put the housing together.  This is done by carefully pushing 
the brushes, one at a time, back against the spring until you actually push the brushes out the 
back of the guide slightly.  At this point the spring will go from pressing on the back of the 
brush to pushing on the side of the brush, holding it nicely in place for assembly.  After the 
alternator is assembled, the brushes may be snapped into place one at a time.  They are 
accessible through a hole adjacent to the connectors for the car wire harness.

1.  See page 123 of the Haynes 914 manual, or the factory shop manual for an exploded view 
of the alternator.

Received: from KGNVMY by vnet.ibm.com (IBM VM SMTP V2R2) with BSMTP id 5783;
   Mon, 08 Jun 92 09:07:22 EDT
Date: Mon, 8 Jun 92 09:07:56 EDT
From: "George Beuselinck" <gb@vnet.ibm.com>
To: porschephiles@karazm.math.uh.edu
Subject: Steering Wheel Removal/Replacement

Removing the steering wheel on an early 944 is easy.

You need a 24 or 28 mm socket (I forget which) probably on a half-inch
drive socket with a 3 inch extension.

First, remove the center cushion.  Use your fingers to get between the
cushion and the wheel itself.  Depending on the wheel, it has either
three or four retainers (they kind of clip in).  This takes a good
amount of force to pop them loose, but it will work.

Next, mark the relative positions of the steering wheel and the
steering column.  I use a punch to notch the steering column at the
point which is the center top of the steering wheel.
Make sure that you do not mark the nut, because, after all, it will
not be attached to the column when you re-align the wheel.

Next, remove the  ignition key to lock the steering column in place.

Use the aforementioned socket, extension and ratchet to loosen and
remove the nut.  Watch out when the nut loosens; if your ratchet
handle is too long, you just might break your windshield.

With the nut and washer off the column, the wheel should slide
off easily.

Replacement is left as an exercise for the student.

> First, remove the center cushion.  Use your fingers to get between the
> cushion and the wheel itself.  Depending on the wheel, it has either
> three or four retainers (they kind of clip in).  This takes a good
> amount of force to pop them loose, but it will work.

Be a little careful about popping the center pad off the steering wheel.  The
retainers are hard plastic and can crack if you generate any shearing motion
when removing the pad.  I once got one side of the pad loose 'unexpectedly'
(it does take a bit of force to pop it) and sort of ripped the other two clips
off.  Since the clips also hold the springs for the horn in compression, the
pad just sort of dangles on the wheel if any one of them is broken... boo

Subject: 944 horn (was Re: Steering Wheel ...)


There have been some good discussions on removing the steering wheel 
without damage. I believe that the original poster mentioned that the
problem was that the horn only honked with the steering wheel in 
selected positions. This makes me think that the problem is related
to a problem a friend of mine with an 84 944 has. His horn never
honks, and we have tracked it to the lack of a ground to the splined
shaft on which the steering wheel mounts (the horn button connects
the nominally grounded shaft to a wire, which then is connected to the
horn through a slip ring which you see when the wheel is removed).
In looking at my friend's car with the steering wheel and the various
turn signal and wiper switches removed, it is not very clear how the
steering shaft is supposed to be grounded. There appear to be some
concentric copper-looking rings where the steering shaft enters the
steering lock assembly, but we don't at present see what wipes against
what to ground the steering shaft. We did verify that if we connect the
steering shaft to the lock assembly temporarily with a clip-lead,
the horn works fine. Has anyone had a similar problem, and found the
solution?

Frank Kerfoot
fwk@homxc.att.com

Sean,

Yes, your solution makes sense.  Although earlier 911 syncro's tend to go away, a problem with first and reverse is likely to be a clutch freeplay adjustment problem.  This is the adjustment you described.  Many supposed transmission problems are actually clutch adjustment problems in disguise. 

There is one more clutch adjustment on most cars, called the pedal travel adjustment.  I recommend you break out the books and do both adjustments to make sure things are right. 

Gary

Why would you double-clutch a car?!

I can understand it with my '66 911 that the 1st and 2nd gear synchros have
since long departed, but on a car with good synchros, it is more of a
hiderance than a help. And what kind of auto-x's do you attend that mandate
a shift? Are you driving a car with a super large ring gear that puts
the spread between very small (e.g. an auto-x specific car)?
I love auto-xing my 911, but have never had to shift. Then again, around
here (S.F. Bay Area) the spots we get are pretty small and hence we get
pretty tight courses.

**************************************************************************

Dave, 

My experience is that the syncros last longer if you double clutch.  
When you consider that you are not requiring them to do as much work,
this may make sense.  I've never owned a new car, so...  Most of them
have had one bad syncro that needed to be "helped."

Most auto-x 's don't require any gear changes (once in second, anyway).
However, some do and if you want to be able to get into first w/o nasty
grinding noises, I recommend practicing.

My observation is that one does autocrossing what one during daily driving,
i.e. you revert to what is most familiar when under stress.  So...
I double clutch on the street, I heel-toe on the street, and I use the an 
auto-x hand technique ("Zarcone" (sp!) method) on the street.  Sometimes 
I'll even late apex, but that's rare, since the turn-in isn't necessary 
and **really** feels strange unless I'm at speed (not to mention what it 
does to your passengers).

Actually, I checked last weekend, and I only D.C. about 70% of the time.

Philip -

Hi there.  The tech bulletins are available as either an expensive subscription,
by which you get them "real time" as issued.  Or in year-end compendia, for $20
to $30 a pop.  The part numbers are:

  Book  Bulletin Issue Date  Part No.     Date
    A   Prior to 11/84       PNA 000 056  10/87
    B   11/84 - 12/85        PNA 000 028   1/86
    C    1/86 - 12/86        PNA 000 055   1/87
    D    1/87 - 12/87        PNA 000 062   1/88
    E    1/88 - 12/88        PNA 000 076   1/89
    F    1/89 - 12/89        PNA 000 082   2/90
    G    1/90 - 12/90        PNA 000 082A  2/91
    H    1/91 - 12/91        PNA 000 082B  2/92

The Service Bulletin Index is PNA 000 018D, dated 3/92, and is a very handy
pocket-size index to all bulletins through 12/91.

The part numbers and title pages would lead me to believe that they are
published by PCNA (Porsche Cars North America), not PAG.  I am certain that a US
dealer's parts department would be happy to sell them to an overseas customer. 
I asked my parts guy (Mac Mackenzie at Clair Parts Express, Dedham MA, fastest
fiche west of the Elbe), and he would be happy to have your business.

The "Upfixin der Porsche" series is published by the Porsche Club of America
(PCA).  What we do is, every few years, collate all the technical articles from
our national mag into a new volume.  You can buy these volumes from Ruth Harte
and the friendly folks at our national office: 703 922 9300, eastern time.  The
articles are aimed at club members, rather than professional mechanics, and are
generally a lot more accessible.

As ever, I'm happy to forward to you photocopies of anything that will help you
with your sunroof.

Kevin

Subject: Re: 911/912 Workshop Manuals

Ron,

I have both the 911 Factory manuals (Vol 1 and 2) and the full 914 Factory manuals.  The Factory manuals are great, although the translation is sometimes a little shakey.  The procedure on CV joint reassembly is a good example of this.
The factory manuals are very expensive, however.  The primary difference between factory and all other manuals is the discussion of why and how things work.  The factory manuals also have more detail on the procedures.  They assume you have the specialized tools, however, whereas Haynes manuals sometimes give suggestions on work-arounds for folks that don't have the tools.  

The best place to just see parts breakdowns is in the factory parts manual.

I also use a Haynes manual and I have a Chilton's manual for the old 911's.  Haynes is much better than Chilton's and is small enough to keep in the car for emergencies.  The factory manuals are a little large for that.

Gary

Date: Tue, 9 Jun 92 09:52:04 PDT
From: dorr@hyperion.llnl.gov (Milo Dorr)
Message-Id: <9206091652.AA07520@hyperion.llnl.gov>
To: porschephiles@karazm.math.uh.edu
Subject: 944 horn (was Re: Steering Wheel ...)

>[Frank]:
>In looking at my friend's car with the steering wheel and the various
>turn signal and wiper switches removed, it is not very clear how the
>steering shaft is supposed to be grounded. There appear to be some

Just a piece of info that may or may not be relevant to the solution
of this puzzle.  A few weeks ago I had to replace the steering rack on
my '86 951.  One of the steps in this operation was the removal of a
small ground wire that electrically connects the rack to the
crossmember (although the rack bolts to the crossmember, it rides in
some rubber bushings).  It is pretty hard to imagine that this could
be the ground path for something as important as the horn circuit,
since the current would have to go through the universal joints on the
steering column, which does not seem like the most realiable
electrical connection (i.e., the connection might be better in some
wheel positions than others, depending on how the pieces of the joint
are making contact, dirt in the joint, etc.).  Nevertheless, if it is
not obvious how the column is grounded, that would seem to make
grounding through the rack a possibility to explore.  You might check
for continuity between the column and rack, then rack and ground.
Since the rack is supposed to be grounded and the column bolts to the
rack, I would think this should be *a* ground path, if not the main
one, for the horn circuit.

Let us know what you find,
-Milo

The local(Northeast) chapter of the PCA recently had a 944 tech
session at a local independent repair shop and the subject of
horn grounding was mentioned. I believe that, in fact, the
u-joints in the steering do ground the horn and can cause the
horn to malfunction when they(u-joints) become loose. It was also
mentioned that the grounding wire occasionally becomes disconnected.

>I'm looking for data on failure of 944 steel front control arms.  I know of two
>instances where folks have told me that they experienced this failure on the
>race track.  What I want to know is:

>- How long do we expect the steel control arm to last on the track before

Don't wait.  Replace them before going to the track.  If you need PCNA doc
backdating or whatever SCCA rigamarole requires, let me know.  (Did the same
last year for someone out west who wanted to put Carrera tensioners in a car.)

>- Where does the failure occur?  Is it possible to detect an imminent failure
>visually before it happens?

Usually in the really fast downhill sweeper.

>- What can be done to strengthen the arm to preclude failure (other than the
>obvious change to the cast arm)?

I know that there is a tradition of welding up reinforcements to 911 and 914
suspension pieces, and see no reason this couldn't also be done to steel 944
arms.  Not sure it's worth the bother.

>- I understand from a reliable source that the cast arms have their problems
>also.  Any information on the failure of cast arms or ball joints would also be
>appreciated.

The wear-items with which I am familiar in the cast front arms are the eventual
wear of the ball joints (non-replaceable) and the bushings at the trailing point
(replaceable).  I am not familiar with any catastrophic failures, except for
Dick Howe's shattered spindle last year (extreme car, extreme case).

Kevin

  Hello, I am trying to avoid making an expensive mistake...
   Has anyone out there had any doings with the chassis stiffening kit
  (weltmeister) for 914's?
   The kit consists of 8 peices of FLAT steel with punched out holes for
  welding onto the body in strategic places that are known to flex under
  stress.  Half of the stiffening plates require some shaping and forming
  to the body contours -not a problem.  But why, oh why would someone want
  to pay nearly $200 for flat steel (0.055") with a few holes in it that
  has to "heat and beat" to make fit?
   Has anyone got a pattern for these peices, or does anyone know the
locations
  on the body these will be placed so I can fabricate from my own dimensions?

   After doing over my rusted out 914 body -flat sheets of patches is really
  easy to do with my air nibbler.  There have been many times where i had to
  make a funny shaped patch to repair a panel or segment that was either too
  expensive, or was not available.  Repairing a body this way was like 
  constructing a 914 from postage stamp size bits of steel.  I am quite sure
  that i could build a 914 from a box of factory parts and do the job quicker
  than i did the restore.

    Could someone please respond to this?  I just got on to this special
   group and i am not sure that what i send out is actually getting out!

I think the best picture of how this stuff gets installed is in
Volume IV of "Upfixin' Der Porsche" from PCA. And that's a pretty
shitty picture of Richie Ginther's car from the early '70s. Brett Johnson's 
guide to authenticity has some pictures but not clear ones.

You might be able to get some additional help from Automechanika since
they sell a pre-formed trailing arm stiffner kit and don't believe in the
old heat and beat method...

Personally, for the street car, I decided that it didn't need reinforced.
The track cars, I triangulated,one with sheet steel gussets, the other
with chromemoly tubing. Big win. Highly non-original. Sigh.

Kyle,

I don't see why you would want to put a chassis stiffening kit in the car unless you are going to spend lots of time on the race track.  I have been racing and maintaining two 914's in a class that does not allow chassis stiffening or triangulation and have not found a major problem with it, contrary to what others say.  I did experience cracking in the area over the rear suspension on the right side, but  I think that had more to do with an airborne assault on one of the guard rails at Lime Rock that left me in the middle of the track with the rest of the field about to run over me.  

I recently had the opportunity to inspect a reinforced trailing arm.  I'm sure it wouldn't break or bend, but you would not believe how heavy it was.  Certainly the trailing arms bend, but the fix is simple - change the trailing arm when you can no longer achieve appropriate camber or toe settings.  I average 1.5 seasons per trailing arm replacement, with a season consisting of nominally 12 races.  Of course we don't use slicks, but the sticky tires we do use are capable of rather high cornering loads.

My advice - save your money, don't buy stiffening kits unless runnning in EP or a GT class (or using slicks), and have a set of spare trailing arms around.

Gary (ITA 2.0 914, NARRC #25)

On a related note I thought people would be interested in knowing what
changes have been made to Watkins Glen for '92. The short answer is "none,
yet." So far this year the changes at the end of the back straight have not
been used for any races or track events. The work on the new section is
still being completed, however, and the grandstands have not been built.
The general, unconfirmed, feeling was that the new "suitcase handle"
(right-left-left-right) section might only ever be used for Nascar, GTP,
and other highly modified races (ala Lime Rock's modified course). In any
case if this section is ever used its entrance is very tight (one car or
two "half cars" with wreckage) and very slow (40MPH, if that, for "street
cars"). The exit is very near turn 5; there would be no way to reach
maximum speed in Turn 5 (~90MPH) until the middle/end of the turn.

Subject:      Air Conditioner

In conversations with Gary Cooke, (otherwise known as the president of the
West Coast 931 Owners' Support Group),  ;-)   we have discovered a flaw in the
A/C of our 931's. I expect it occurs in other models also. When the A/C is
turned on, the fans which cool not only the engine coolant, but also the A/C
condenser, should be locked on full speed. (With the A/C switched off, the fans
rely on a thermal switch to power them on as needed).
If they do not switch on with the A/C, the high pressure side of the freon
system will pop up to EXCESSIVE pressures when the car is not moving and the
fans are not running. Exploding refrigerant lines, can prove exciting, as Gary
has attested, especially if they are inside the car. (Much like a shot gun
going off beside you).
Both of our 931's developed leaks at the sight glass/receiver. Is there a
commonly occurring cause for this problem? ie. any particular relay that
frequently fails or other electrical weak spot? ken.

  Ken Stanton,   Doctoral Program, Sch. of Business,
                 Queen's U., Kingston, Canada K7L 3N6
                 Tel:(613)545-2377

To: porschephiles@bcm.tmc.edu
Subject: dash lights


recently, i discovered an interesting phenomenon with at least one
of the "idiot lights" that can appear on a porsche 928 dash.  when
brake failure occurs, a relay is tripped and the "brake press" light
comes on.  simple enough, except that the relay remains tripped as
long as there is power to it.  in order to reset the relay, you have
to pull the battery cable.

for those keeping score at home, i related a tale about "the bearing
bummer" at about this time last year in which an inner wheel bearing
had broken wreaking havoc and trashing a brake caliper (catastrophic
brake failure).  after fixing things, the light remained on, and i
posted several questions to the list asking for advice on what to
check.  thanks again to one and all, and especially milo, without
whose help, i'd still be walking.  it turns out, all i had to do was
pull the battery cable.  so, i think we have finally reached the happy
ending of a long story: i no longer have to look at the big red "!" and
"brake press" lights while driving...  cuts down on embarrassing
questions, too.  :-)


cheers,

bert

Original-From: homxc!fwk (F W Kerfoot, Iii +1 908 949 6348)
To: porschephiles@math.UH.EDU
Subject: Late 944 front control arm failure

>>- How long do we expect the steel control arm to last on the track before
>>failure?
>
>Don't wait.  Replace them before going to the track.  If you need PCNA doc
>backdating or whatever SCCA rigamarole requires, let me know.  (Did the same
>last year for someone out west who wanted to put Carrera tensioners in a car.)

There appears to be more than one "conventional wisdom" on that subject.
I believe that some racers believe that reinforced versions of the old
steel arms are fine. My recollection is that the school cars (944) used
by the Akin-White racing school use reinforced steel arms.

>I know that there is a tradition of welding up reinforcements to 911 and 914
>suspension pieces, and see no reason this couldn't also be done to steel 944
>arms.  Not sure it's worth the bother.

See below-

>
>>- I understand from a reliable source that the cast arms have their problems
>>also.  Any information on the failure of cast arms or ball joints would also be
>>appreciated.
>
>The wear-items with which I am familiar in the cast front arms are the eventual
>wear of the ball joints (non-replaceable) and the bushings at the trailing point
>(replaceable).  I am not familiar with any catastrophic failures, except for
>Dick Howe's shattered spindle last year (extreme car, extreme case).
>
Bob Strange (successful SCCA racer of 944 Turbo's) claims there is a
catastrophic failure associated with the new arms, where the shaft of the
ball joint fails where it enters the strut. You can (and should, if you run
many track events) loosen the clamp on the strut and slide the ball joint
shaft out a bit, and look for cracks in the shaft.

The interesting part is that you can't replace the ball joint separately
in the new, cast arms. Therefore, we have moved from periodic replacement
of a relatively inexpensive steel arm to periodic replacement of a
relatively expensive cast alloy arm (in Bob's opinion).

Frank Kerfoot
fwk@homxc.att.com

Message-Id: <9206141710.AA08624@jeeves.UCSD.EDU>
To: porschephiles@karazm.math.uh.edu
Subject: RE: It was bound to happen... 

I'm sure you have heard it before......If someone wants your car bad enough,
no alarm is going to stop them.  That being said, there is a lot you can do.
Some of the things I've learned since my Sciroc was stolen 6' form where
I slept.
1) Never get an alarm with a delay. Even 30 sec is enough time for most people
   to get past most alarms.
2) Battery back-up is a good idea.
3) Try not to mount the alarm brain in an obvious place like under the dash.
4) If you park your car on the street at night, get an alarm that flashes the
   lights or one that has a small $5 strobe that you can mount under your car.
   I've seen a car alarm with one of the strobes and it was very noticeable
   to say the least.
5) The best option I got with my new alarm is a "field sensor". I have it 
   hidden in my ash tray and it will trip the alarm if anything comes within
   about 3'. So even if you have your sunroof off and someone just reaches
  into your car the alarm will go off. Most likely you can retrofit most alarms
   with this sensor, and they only cost around $100.

If you need more info I'll dig out my receipts and find out exactly who makes
the field sensor.  

Blaine Stine
stine@jeeves.ucsd.edu

Christopher R. Zach says:
> Anyway, it seems that there is a chink in the Porsche alarm system, ie 
> prying open the roof. While they could not take the car, the thought tha
> any moron with a screwdriver can get into my car without setting the alarm
> really bothers me. Is there a way to link the auto alarm to the sunroof
> switch? Is the sunroof that easy to pry open?
> 
> If not/so, what is a good alarm system that will stop the thief before he 
> gets into the car?

Instead of adding a switch to the sunroof, you might look into getting a
motion sensor.  I have one of these in my convertable.  Its adjustable for
area, I have it set so that you can touch the windows, but if you bat the
roof or stick something in the read window the alarm goes off.  I don't have
a shock sensor or a glass break microphone, since its a convertable, just
the radar motion sensor and switches on the doors, trunk and engine.  Very
few false alarms this way and when it goes off I know someone is inside.
The best part is I can leave the top down and the alarm will still function
the same way.

--

At the Stoddard swap meet this past weekend, I had lots of opportunities
to BS with all kinds of enthusiasts.  One conversation in particular
related to Solex Carbs.  He mentioned that to properly adjust the
floats, you need some kind of $90 float adjustment tool and...... 

He recommended the following:
	1.  take carbs off car.
	2.  construct a 1/2" in diameter 7'2" in length hose and connect
		it to the gas input
	3.  fill this hose w/gas
	4.  adjust the float using the $90 fload adjustment tool.
	5.  reinstall

Allegedly, he says you need the 7'2" hose to put the appropriate
amount of pressure on the carb to adjust the floats correctly.  I
can only imagine him filling that hose in his basement in the
winter, think of the fumes!  He also said the carbs are soooo sensitive
that 1mm off can make the car run hot (too lean) or flood (too rich)?
Further, he also said you cant adjust them on the car.

Okay, so what gives?  Is this guy nuts, or did I get some
great info.  I'm having enough trouble getting the gas tank out (rust
on this ridiculous piece of sheet metal underneath the gas tank)
to get it rebuilt, I really dont want to start making a 7'2" hose up

From stan Mon Jun 15 10:35:10 1992

Subject: Re: Solex Carbs.....
To: rondl@ihlpy.att.com
Date: Mon, 15 Jun 92 15:35:10 CDT
Cc: porschephiles@karazm.math.uh.edu
In-Reply-To: <199206151845.AA03984@math.UH.EDU>; from "rondl@ihlpy.att.com" at Jun 15, 92 1:43 pm
X-Mailer: ELM [version 2.3 PL11]

His story is essentially accurate, but there are other ways to handle
this. I had a jig set up with a 5 gallon plastic fuel cell (from another
project), an electric fuel pump and an adjustable pressure regulator.

And a funny little piece of metal that looked like an intake manifold
mounting flange welded to a piece of angle iron.

Bolt carb to bracket, install bracket in vise. Hook up gas plumbing
and turn on the pump. Adjust pressure regulator to correct value (depends
on application). Pop top on carb, adjust float level. Viola! Done.

Some Weber and Dellorto carbs are similarly sensitive. Particularly the 
Weber progressives. Bleah. This is, by the way, akin to adjusting the
pressure monitor on an FI car. You wouldn't complain about having to 
do that, you'd just do it. So, just do it.

Stan

Stan -

You moved a decimal point, which made looking up the part take five minutes
instead of 30 seconds for my friend the Bosch CSE!  The original part on an '81
Euro 911SC is 0438.140.069, not .039.  The .039 part was likely substituted
during federalization because it chows less gas, keeping those all-important
numbers down.  This was a pretty common thing to do in federalizing.

The original part (.069) is available.  List is $300, but a cheque for $225 sent
to Jerry will liberate one for you.  Send it along to:

  Jerry Pellegrino
  European Performance Engineering
  10 Cochituate Street
  Natick, MA  01760
  508 651 1316

Mention my name and the part number.  I have no financial interest except as
limited to keeping Jerry in the black so's we can go to track events and split
hotel room bills...

By the by, Jerry is a Bosch CSE and can apparently lay his hands on otherwise
hard-to-get Bosch parts.

Kevin

Frank -

>There appears to be more than one "conventional wisdom" on that subject.
>I believe that some racers believe that reinforced versions of the old
>steel arms are fine. My recollection is that the school cars (944) used
>by the Akin-White racing school use reinforced steel arms.

Indeed.  There must be something pithy that can be said about my knowledge being
more conventional than wise!  I have no first-hand experience with the steel
arms or their failures.

>Bob Strange (successful SCCA racer of 944 Turbo's) claims there is a
>catastrophic failure associated with the new arms, where the shaft of the
>ball joint fails where it enters the strut. You can (and should, if you run
>many track events) loosen the clamp on the strut and slide the ball joint
>shaft out a bit, and look for cracks in the shaft.

>The interesting part is that you can't replace the ball joint separately
>in the new, cast arms. Therefore, we have moved from periodic replacement
>of a relatively inexpensive steel arm to periodic replacement of a
>relatively expensive cast alloy arm (in Bob's opinion).

True.  I saw stamped steel arms listed for <$30 today, aluminum for >$300.  It's
also the case that polyurethane bushings are not available for the front
944-series suspension with the new arms.  I called Automotion today.  They are
developing them, and the bushings should be available in a few months time.  The
original rubber bushings at the trailing edge of the control arm (where the
caster is set) deteriorate with age.

Kevin

go with the bars!

Christopher -

Sorry to hear about this.  You can certainly integrate the sunroof into the
factory alarm circuitry.  There is a "roof closed" microswitch.  It may take
adding a new relay.  I'll try to puzzle it out with the wiring diagrams it as
time permits - I don't have a quick answer.

It is also possible to gets the car's lamps to flash with the alarm horn.  In
fact, this was an option added in MY 89, option M484 if memory serves.  I have
just about finished upgrading my '87 944S factory alarm to this option by
acquiring the relay, socket, and connector pins from my parts guy (Mac at Clair
Parts Express).  It's a simple matter of taking the strobed (except in
Switzerland - no joke!) alarm signal from the alarm control unit, and using it
to drive the new relay.  The relay switches the fog lamps and rear parking
lights.

In fact, the (almost) last piece went in this evening after I was given the
weird female pin used in the relay socket for the small (2.8 mm) male pins - a
miniature version of the four-tonque, spring-type connectors used in the CEB
connector blocks.  I'll get its part number from Mac tomorrow and should then be
able to write up a how-to article.

I've never heard if anyone bothering to go in through the sunroof before.  Since
the windows are not alarmed, most thieves will just punch the driver-side
window.

Kevin

Subject: 3.2L Upgrade

When I rebuilt my 911SC engine, I wanted to get the 98mm pistons which
would have given me 3.2L. Besides the fact that they are expensive, the
only drawback for me was the fact that the heads need machining to
accomodate the cylinders. I found this quite surprising because the
911 Performance Handbook never mentioned this fact. I found out by
inquiring at Andial for the P & C set. I did not want to machine the
heads in case I needed to revert back to the 95mm pistons.

In the end, I went for the 9.8 CR 95mm pistons and am happy with the
results. I figure I have around 200-205 HP with these pistons and the
SSI exhaust in my 79 911SC. I reckon with the 3.2 conversion, you can
get around 220 HP and much better torque. Can anyone else comment if
the heads really do need machining?

Cheers

Sugi

Subject: Re: Rollbars again

Steve Tynor asks about roll bars for his 944.

My personal opinion is that a roll bar is only an incremental  
improvement over no protection.  Based somewhat on personal and  
anecdotal evidence, but mostly on professional experience with  
materials and structures (I have a PhD in mechanical engineering), I  
would suggest going all the way to a roll cage.  A roll bar will only  
protect you from some rollover accidents, will not protect you from  
most rollovers where the front of the roof is impacted, nor from side  
or front impacts.  Recall those accidents you've seen at the track  
and ask yourself whether you'd rather have a roll cage or roll bar in  
each case.

For a relatively heavy car like the 944, I would suggest following  
the SCCA recommendation of 1.75" x 0.120" DOM tubing of mild steel.   
Alloy and/or heat-treated steel tends to be more brittle as well as  
stronger, as well as being more difficult to weld correctly.

I have a roll cage from Kirk Racing Products in my 944 SCCA ITS race  
car.  It cost about $465 and was delivered about 10 days from when I  
placed the order over the phone.  The tubing is DOM mild steel, 1.75"  
dia by 0.120" wall, and it is legal for all SCCA and PCA stock  
classes.  It is relatively unobstrusive, requires no cutting of  
interior trim (other than carpet) and the door bar can be easily  
removed for street driving if you wish.  I don't drive my car on the  
street, though.

Call (205)823-6025 and ask for Al.  He will know my name if you  
mention it, though I have no relationship with the business other  
than as a satisfied customer.

Nathan
ulrich@canberra.whoi.edu

1986 911 Carrera (street car); 1985/1 944 (ITS race car)

Concerning Safety Devices Roll Cages:

I've seen two Safety Devices Roll Cages for 914's.  It makes me think that someone at the company either can't read, or didn't thoroughly read (more likely) the SCCA GCR.  The cage they were selling for the 914 did not meet GCR requirements in that all components of the cage were not made of the same tubing.  The rear supports are flat tabs that bolt into the shoulder harness holes vice tubing as required by SCCA.  I don't have any problem with the stength of the cage, but it's a hassle to have to pay good money to modify an already expensive product to comply with existing rules.  The funny thing is, if you call Dick Martin at SCCA, he'll tell you that the cage is OK as is, but won't give it to you in writing.  I modified the cage to comply with GCR.

The moral is - whenever you buy a product for racing, don't take anyone's word that it's legal without verifying yourself.

Gary

Sean,

I was deficient (and was reminded of it) in not mentioning the master cylinder switch.  Certainly if the brake failure switch is defective such that it's always shut and can't be reset, then the light won't go away.  As was pointed out, most of these cars have a reset for this switch located under the rubber grommet on the master cylinder, beside where the wire plugs in.  To reset, just push in on the little plastic point sticking out.  It should stay in when reset.  
In my car's case, the light flashes even when this wire is unplugged, which prompted my comments.

Gary

From: John Posthill <jbp%ranger@rti.rti.org>
Message-Id: <9206161928.AA08784@ranger.rti.org>
To: porschephiles@karazm.math.uh.edu, school@cabot.balltown.cma.com,
        tynor@prism.gatech.edu, wheeltowheel@abingdon.eng.sun.com
Subject: Re:  Rollbars again

STEVE TYNOR writes:
*Whilst calling around trying to find a deal on a bolt-in Autopower (AP)
*roll bar for my 944 turbo, I talked with a dealer who said he refused to
*sell them any more since there had been a couple of recent failures (as
*I understand it, the AP bar is made of chrome-moly and is harder (hence
*more brittle) than cold rolled steel and when it reaches its stress
*point, cracks rather than bends. I assume that AP uses CrMo for weight
*savings (anyone know how much lighter than a steel bar they typically
*are?).

Where are the rollbars cracking and, more importantly, under what
conditions?  ``Chrome-moly'' _is_ 4130 steel and is considerably higher
strength than your plain carbon steel like 1020 to 1040.  It is not
that it is lighter per se, but you can use less and get the same
strength, or use the same amount and get higher strength.  As for
cracking rather than bending - yes, generally the higher strength
steels will not deform as much as the lower strength steels before
ultimate failure.  Without knowing any of the important details, I
think this cracking info needs to be treated like a major league
red flag.  I would not want to buy one until I knew enough about
what is what.

Some things that could cause cracking that come to mind:
1.  They used 4140 (or higher C content material) by accident and their 
    welding process left some microcracks that were just waiting to 
    open up at an inopportune time.     :-(
2.  They screwed up the welding very badly (of course, the cracks
    would need to be at the welds for both of these scenarios.....)
3.  The users really hit something superhard and the structure
    deformed and failed (hard to believe but possible).

If it is your life at stake, I'd spend the bucks to get something that
you are *sure* is going to work.   My 2 nickels worth..............

Regards,
John Posthill

|	Ever since the centre caps with the painted Porsche crest from my
|telephone-dial wheels were stolen :-(, I've been looking at the

I had the black/silver wheels on mine and I decided to paint the crest also.
I used the "Humbrol" enamels (for models) as I have used them for other
purposes before and they are very hardy. 

|2. Do I need to do any special preparation? I planned to just clean

Now you are asking, I forget the paint "numbers" ;-) They are just red, black
and gold. One thing though, after about 1 year after painting I found that the
gold was wearing off. Then I realised what I should have done when I finished
painting was to have put a "clear" coat over the paint when I had finished. If
you do this it will last much longer.

Another hint too, buy a VERY VERY small brush it makes life much easier.

Greg writes:



I am in the process of replacing a few broken head studs and 
performing a top end rebuild to my stock 80 911SC at a friends shop. 
He told me he thinks he can get a good deal on some 3.2 piston
and cylinders :-). 

Is there any drawback to putting in the 3.2s besides more $$$$$?

What class at a PCA autocross would I have to run in?
Modifed SC class or with the Carreras?

Would it be competitive with the Carreras?

One other thing is the motor has 71,000 miles on it and shows
very little wear on the inside.
****************


Greg,

Anderson's book (911 Performance Handbook) doesn't mention any drawbacks.
Sugi mentions that the heads may need machining.  I haven't heard this 
from anyone else, but I don't know anyone that has done this mod.

I drive (auto-x) in zone 7 (ggr) in IP (one class up from stock).  If I 
remember correctly, you are allowed (in IP) to make your car 
"functionally equivalent" to others in the model range.  Since the 3.2 
carreras are in the model range (i think) and use the same transmission, 
you could probably (worst case) carry some extra weight to ballance things 
out.  Of course, these are zone 7 rules and your mileage may vary.

I think there may also be an allowable displacement increase for IP, in
which case you may be okay w/o any extra weight.

You definately will not be able to run as "stock."

If you don't buy the p/c (or more than one set is available) let me know,
as I might like them!


--Soren            laforce@xenon.arc.nasa.gov

Nathan,

These are my recommendations for trying to eliminate a vibration in the steering wheel at 65 MPH.  For the porschephiles, any other comments on this problem (with which I am nearly totally unfamiliar) would be appreciated.  Car is an 85 944.

- Have wheels dynamically balanced (currently only statically balanced)

- Swap fronts to rear and check for change in problem

- Ensure front wheel bearings are properly adjusted (at the tight end of spec)

- Check for play in tie rod ends, ball joint or strut attachments (or anywhere else)

- Ensure toe is properly adjusted, consider slight increase in toe in 

Gary

Date: Wed, 17 Jun 92 09:58:30 EDT
From: "George Beuselinck" <gb@vnet.ibm.com>
To: porschephiles@karazm.math.uh.edu
Subject: 944 Wheel Vibration

Other recommendations:

1) Check out Upfixin Der Porsche (Volume VII, I believe) which has
   recommendations for wheel alignment to minimize this type of
   vibration.  I seem to remember that that the caster get changed to
   +2.5 degrees.  That change made a big difference in sensitivity
   to this problem on my car.

2) Have absolutely, positively, zero-tolerance round wheels and tires.
   No matter how well they are balanced, if they are the least bit
   out-of-round, your steering wheel will shake like crazy.
   This includes flatspots from braking.

3) This problem was supposed to have only been really noticeable
   on early 944s, without power-steering.

4) Don't drive 65 (drive 85 or 90, like God intended you to!)  :-)

George Beuselinck  (HVR PCA 944 Bigot)  1992/06/17 10:04 EDT

From: LAFORCE@xenon.arc.nasa.gov
