Subject: pca zone 7 rules on 911sc 3.0 to 3.2
To: porschephiles@karazm.math.uh.edu


I brought in my rule book (pca zone 7) to check the effects of the 3.0
to 3.2 conversion (95 to 98 mm p/c) on auto-x classes.

Production:

"Cars may be converted to the specifications of another model within its 
model range provided that the converted car is functionally equivalent to 
that model.  Functionally equivalent is defined as being identical
in areas of engine, transmission, suspension, brakes, weight, and other
performance-affecting items."

The 3.2 carrera produces 207 hp; the 3.0, 180 hp.  I believe that the 
brakes and suspension are the same.  Both use the 915 transmission.  The
SC weighs about 2750 lb; I don't know the weight of the 3.2.

The model range is "911 1978 on," the next model range is the carrera 2,
so both are in the same model range.

So, for zone 7, you would have a good chance of staying in production
as long as your car weighed the same as a 3.2 (or carried extra weight?).


Super Production:

Cars that are production but have additional modifications (allowable
mods are specified).  One of the mods is: "Non-stock pistons and/or 
cylinders, with up to a 5mm increased bore."


If I interpret the rules correctly, you would be production if the car
weighs the same as a 3.2, and super production if it is lighter.


--Soren            laforce@xenon.arc.nasa.gov

On Jun 17,  9:28am, desousa@cisco.com writes:
> I'm curious about which injection system you're considering?
> 
> Like out of another car?  Will the car be streetable too? 

Not straight out of another car, no.  I don't know yet.  There are numerous
aftermarket possibilities, like Haltech and Weber, though the Weber system
is currently only for American V8's.  Then there are more generic systems,
like Bosch Motronic and LH-Motronic.  I prefer these because they are
pulsed, and because they also control the spark.  However, converting these
to work on the 914 will probably be challenging, as a result of (1) the
number of inputs, and (2) the difficulty in changing the computer
parameters.  I don't happen to have a PROM burner handy, though there must
be one around here somewhere (I *do* work in an EE department!)

I discussed the idea of taking one out of another car recently with someone
else.  This is generally unfeasable.  The problem is that things like intake
shape, injector size and performance, engine size and performance, cam
profile, you name it will affect the parameters of the injection system.
You've got to be prepared to change *everything*, from the computer itself
to the injectors, intake manifolds, throttles, everything.

Mark

>> Not straight out of another car, no.  I don't know yet.  There are numerous
>> aftermarket possibilities, like Haltech and Weber, though the Weber system
>> is currently only for American V8's.  Then there are more generic systems,
>> like Bosch Motronic and LH-Motronic.  I prefer these because they are
>> pulsed, and because they also control the spark.  However, converting these
>> to work on the 914 will probably be challenging, as a result of (1) the
>> number of inputs, and (2) the difficulty in changing the computer
>> parameters.  I don't happen to have a PROM burner handy, though there must
>> be one around here somewhere (I *do* work in an EE department!)
>> 
>> I discussed the idea of taking one out of another car recently with someone
>> else.  This is generally unfeasable.  The problem is that things like intake
>> shape, injector size and performance, engine size and performance, cam
>> profile, you name it will affect the parameters of the injection system.
>> You've got to be prepared to change *everything*, from the computer itself
>> to the injectors, intake manifolds, throttles, everything.
>> 
>> Mark

fast, economical, and reliable.  

The injection system in the 914 I have now is a little tired but still 
functions well.

On Jun 17, 11:15am, desousa@cisco.com writes:
> Maybe the injection system that is on there now can be modified to be
> computer controlled to improve the old design?  At least you wouldn't
> have to start from scratch? 

The limit on the stock Bosch D-Jetronic (914 1.7 and 2.0) is not in the
computer.  I know of people who have put a Haltech computer in and kept
everything else the same, and saw minimal gain.  I don't know where the loss
is, but it's not in the computer.  Probably in the air intake; that would be
my guess.  Possibly in the fuel delivery system itself -- D-Jet is a
high-pressure system, and is prone to fuel leaks.  I dunno, but I definitely
want something else.  Something low-pressure, something individually pulsed,
something light and simple to work on (once it's in), and preferably
something that also controls ignition.

Mark

Subject:What rims and tires for my Porsche 944?

Ed, if you ever plan on running a track event anywhere in the
Northeast, DON'T USE AFTERMARKET WHEELS.

The track chairs and the zone safety chairs are pretty much in
agreement that Porsche factory wheels (and perhaps BBS)
are acceptable for the kind of driving that we see.

If I may promote my favorite set of books again (Upfixin der Porsche),
there is a great picture of an aftermarket wheel which has had the
center broken out of it.

If you're never going on the track, never going to drive more than
55, never going to hit a pothole or a curb, then MSW wheels are exactly
what you need (but put them on your Mazda Miata, not your 944)...

(My personal experience with MSW wheels goes back 9 years, when I
bought 4 to replace wheels on my Audi coupe;  Two years later, I
bought 4 BBS wheels, which stayed on the car for the next 7 years.
The MSW wheels were almost as susceptible to bending as the original
Audi cast wheels....JUNK!)

Do it right the first time, or don't waste the time or money doing it
at all.  Original 15 inch wheels are better than junk 16 inch wheels
anytime.

Subject: Re: Weltmeiter Chips

Charles -

I think that the answer depends on how much you are willing to pay for the
performance increase.  In turbo-charged cars, the chips can typically make a
very big difference.  They can also make a big difference in the longevity of
the head gasket, etc., if done improperly.  In normally-aspirated cars, the
gains from aftermarket chips seem minimal, from my own experience and from all
other credible reports.  (Dissenting opinions are sought, anyone?)

I put an AutoThority version 2.5 chip in my 944S only because someone spent a
lot of money on it, then decided to sell his car.  The new owner didn't want the
chip, so the original owner sold it to me for a lot less.  There seem to be
plenty of people in this situation - I would recommend that anyone looking for a
chip try first to find a "used" one.  I checked with AutoThority before
purchase, and they were happy to transfer warranty and upgrade privileges to me.

If you do decide to get the chip, go with a known product that other people have
already successfully used.  AutoThority is such a product, although there were
problems with earlier versions of their stuff.

If you are not handy with electronic devices, and even if you are, it's probably
a good idea to have a knowledgeable friend help you install the chip.  Wear a
static-discharge cuff while you do it (or wrap a wire around your wrist and
connect it to the DME box - same thing).

But I would really recommend that you first spend this kind of money on:

  - driving schools,
  - tires,
  - sways bars,

and probably a number of other items before moving on to chipping the car.

Kevin

Greg -

As far as legality goes, it's entirely up to the region to decide.  They often
pattern their classes after the PCR's.  The Parade Competition Rules (PCR's) are
used for the annual, national meet.  They are, uh, far from perfect.

A lot of regions make up classes and decide legality based on the population of
local cars.  For example, we (NER) created a class this year just for Carrera
with a popular set of modifications (twin-plug, etc.).  We have a bunch of
people who had this set-up, who wanted to autocross, but were discouraged at the
thought of being dumped into a catch-all class.

Example: my 944-series car is entirely legal in NER, legal at the Zone and
National levels if I change my rear tires, and is totally frowned upon for any
of a long list of reasons in SCCA competition.

Kevin

Date: Wed, 17 Jun 92 15:31:31 PDT
From: bobq@tc.fluke.com (Robert A. Quarterman)
To: porschephiles@karazm.math.uh.edu
Subject: 914 Injection System

  The 914 injection system is more easily modified than the newer CPU
based systems. The 914 injection system is an analog computer that uses
resistor values and an amplifier to vary the pulse width for the injectors.
  If you vary the resistor values, to change the pulse width, you can change
the fuel delivery curve. The fuel delivery curve can be modified to compensate
for larger displacement, full race cams, an enlarged throttle body and etc. 
  The only short coming are the points in the distributor which can be changed to hall affect to improve reliability.
  In short the tools required to modify an analog computer are a lot less expensive and easier to change than the CPU systems. Oh I forgot to mention that the
electronics in the 914 are circa 1965 design practices, long before 8 bit micro
processors.
  I would be interested in knowing if any one else in this mailing has modified
the stock 914 electronics????

Have Fun
Bob Q.

From: mvmas@mvgpf.att.com (Mark A Skala)
To: porschephiles@karazm.math.uh.edu
Subject: 914 flasher relay - solution
Organization: AT&T Network Systems
Reply-To: vwp@cbnewsk.att.com

Last night I found the solution to the clicking relay in the 914.  After
looking more closely at the wiring diagram, the emergency brake contact
switch (which has been removed from my car by a previous owner) seemed
to be connected directly to the light on the dash and then across the
light to get to the flasher to activate it.  So I pulled the gauge with
the fuel level and big red lamp on it, and disconnected one lead from
the lamp.  Now the thing only clicks when necessary.  And, like Gary
says, if you need the warning light to tell you your brakes are bad, you
shouldn't be driving a Porsche.  :)


Mark A. Skala                            "Please conserve paper; write on
vwp@cbnewsk.att.com                       all six sides before recycling."

Hi there. Sorry for the delayed response, but I'm on the Digest list, not
the "immediate" mailing list. For any responses, please include my email
address in the CC list. Anyway, I seem to remember having a similar
discussion on W2W not too long ago. I ended up using the Electromotive
system for several reasons.

1. It provides both injection and ignition.
2. Both VE and spark advance tables can easily be programmed through a PC.
   In addition, you can have several different tables stored on disk, each
   with a different setup and specs, and download whichever one is
   appropriate.
3. For a similar system it's much cheaper than the Haltech and certainly
   than the NOS system. And from conversations I've had with users of
   Haltech, they had lots of problems with it. Please remember, however,
   that this is only according to two Haltech users. It could be that they
   didn't know how to use it properly, that they had different expectations
   from it, etc.
4. I don't have the new version, but there's an article in the latest Turbo
   magazine that describes it. It has charting capabilities, and
   closed-loop tuning. This can make life A LOT easier, believe me.

The only problem I had with the TEC-II unit, as it's called, is that the
reference manual that comes with it is far from clear. Actually I don't
think that there was any direct correspondance between the manual and the
system that was shipped to me! I have to say, though, that they had lots of
on-line help in the program, but to me it's not the same as having a manual
in your hand. Take care.

Subject: chips

18 June

Charles,

Hello.  Regarding chips for 944:  I put an Autothority chip in my 1985.1.  I
can say only that I LOVED IT!! I found that there was noticably more torque
which made both part-throttle and full throttle operation MUCH more pleasant.
I can't tell you quantitatively how much  more power/torque there was, but
I can promise you that it was very noticeable.

I had absolutely no reliability problems, nor any trouble. There is 1 very
important caveat, though: you MUST use gasoline of high-enough octane
rating, or you will get destructive knocking. The 944 chip requires you to
use high-octane gas, versus the low-octane which the un-chipped car uses.

I think the chip is very easy to install even by an amateur.  You remove the
electronic brain from the car with a screwdriver.  You then can take it
indoors and open the brain with a screwdriver.  You GENTLY remove the stock
chip by rocking it a little and/or prying it up with the blade of a
screwdriver, and pop in the new chip. As long as you are CAREFUL and GENTLE
there will be no problem. You then close the brain case and put it back in
the car. Lastly, you drive away with a big smile on your face. 

Implementation suggestion: Put high-octane gas in the car in advance of the
chip conversion, so that you will have only the good gas in the tank!


Fred Pack

Chris -

I have a cover which I use except in the winter.  The car gets driven
year-round, but with salt and sand, covering in the winter isn't practical.  I
use the cover, like you, for bird poop, sunshine, and as a theft deterrant. 
(Question: if you're a thief with a lot full of swell cars, would you waste time
on the covered car?)

I think the cover's a Technalon or whatever it's called, sold by Imparts.  I
haven't had any problems with scratching or from wind, other than getting it on
and off.  Light rain doesn't penetrate it, but really heavy rain will leak a
bit.

I also use a bra.  I had two Colgan three-pieces, now have a Porsche bra and
it's unquestionably the best design.  (From the manufacture and markings, I
think Colgan makes Porsche's bra for them.)  The key is keeping dirt and grit
from between the bra and paint, otherwise you're worse off.  I vacuum it out
every time I take it off to wash the car.  It seals against water pretty well,
so I have found it not necessary to remove it except after driving in heavy
rain.

One hint is to slip small squares of flannel cloth between the bra and the car
at its stitching next to the topmost side clips.  I am not sure whether it is
because of invaded grit or the stitching, but without the flannel scraps I get
scratching.  I got this idea from a 911 owner friend who had the same problem
over his fenders.

I've modified the bra.  It is only available with a license plate cut-out, into
which I stitched a piece from an earlier bra.  I also cut out pieces to allow
air into the dam where it gets fed to the Holbert Cool-Brake, rehemming the
edges with hemming material from the earlier bra.  Piece of cake, structurally
copacetic.

Kevin

Kevin,

I'd be primarily concerned about two things - rust and engine.  These cars have lots of rust problems.  Pay special attention to the front, where the front of the A arms bolt to the chassis,  the battery boxes and spare tire well, the rocker panels and the area where the rear torsion bars enter the chassis.

I think old 911 prices are depressed, so you can get good value for the money.  But!! A 911 engine rebuild can run into $3000 of parts, and the cost to have it done could mean that even if you purchase a car at a great price, you could end up having much more in the car than it's worth.  I would only purchase a 70 T if the engine was rebuilt (and I knew who did the work) or if it still had lots of miles left on it.   Otherwise, the price would have to be very good.  I think a T is a fine car for daily driving or for a starting point for modification.  The 70 series is one of the lightest weight versions.  Watch for worn out transmission syncro`s.

I have a 71 E, so I'm somewhat familiar with these.

Gary

Subject: EFI follow-up

Over the last week there has been some interesting traffic on this list
regarding EFI for the 914. Since I've been doing a lot of work in my
914 EFI and have both built an engine management system for a GT-1 racer
and programmed two different systems for an IMSA GTU 911, I want to speak up
here. 

Two warnings: I'm not an expert. I've just got a lot of "seat time" in 
working on makiing EFI do strange things, and have spent time trying to
reconcile my hands-on findings with the actual physics involved so I can
repeat the performance. Second, this is going to be a *LONG* message. I'm
going to start off with a re-cap of the weeks's traffic on EFI so we all
have it fresh in our minds, then start my info at the line with "*START
OF STAN'S INFO* on it, in case you want to search ahead for it. That's also
113 lines from here in case that works better for you.

From: Mark Sirota <msirota@ee.rochester.edu>
Date: Wed, 17 Jun 92 11:56:39 EDT

... and this winter I plan on replacing the fuel injection with
some other injection system.

Date: Wed, 17 Jun 92 09:28:50 PDT
From: desousa@cisco.com

I'm curious about which injection system you're considering?

Like out of another car?  Will the car be streetable too? 


From: Mark Sirota <msirota@ee.rochester.edu>
Date: Wed, 17 Jun 92 13:38:55 EDT

Not straight out of another car, no.  I don't know yet.  There are numerous
aftermarket possibilities, like Haltech and Weber, though the Weber system
is currently only for American V8's.  Then there are more generic systems,
like Bosch Motronic and LH-Motronic.  I prefer these because they are
pulsed, and because they also control the spark.  However, converting these
to work on the 914 will probably be challenging, as a result of (1) the
number of inputs, and (2) the difficulty in changing the computer
parameters.  I don't happen to have a PROM burner handy, though there must
be one around here somewhere (I *do* work in an EE department!)

I discussed the idea of taking one out of another car recently with someone
else.  This is generally unfeasable.  The problem is that things like intake
shape, injector size and performance, engine size and performance, cam
profile, you name it will affect the parameters of the injection system.
You've got to be prepared to change *everything*, from the computer itself
to the injectors, intake manifolds, throttles, everything.

Date: Wed, 17 Jun 92 11:15:10 PDT
From: desousa@cisco.com

the original injection system that was a blast to drive.  The car was
fast, economical, and reliable.  

Maybe the injection system that is on there now can be modified to be
computer controlled to improve the old design?  At least you wouldn't
have to start from scratch? 

From: Mark Sirota <msirota@ee.rochester.edu>
Date: Wed, 17 Jun 92 14:21:17 EDT

On Jun 17, 11:15am, desousa@cisco.com writes:
> Maybe the injection system that is on there now can be modified to be
> computer controlled to improve the old design?  At least you wouldn't
> have to start from scratch? 

The limit on the stock Bosch D-Jetronic (914 1.7 and 2.0) is not in the
computer.  I know of people who have put a Haltech computer in and kept
everything else the same, and saw minimal gain.  I don't know where the loss
is, but it's not in the computer.  Probably in the air intake; that would be
my guess.  Possibly in the fuel delivery system itself -- D-Jet is a
high-pressure system, and is prone to fuel leaks.  I dunno, but I definitely
want something else.  Something low-pressure, something individually pulsed,
something light and simple to work on (once it's in), and preferably
something that also controls ignition.

Date: Wed, 17 Jun 92 15:31:31 PDT
From: bobq@tc.fluke.com (Robert A. Quarterman)

  The 914 injection system is more easily modified than the newer CPU
based systems. The 914 injection system is an analog computer that uses
resistor values and an amplifier to vary the pulse width for the injectors.
  If you vary the resistor values, to change the pulse width, you can change
the fuel delivery curve. The fuel delivery curve can be modified to compensate
for larger displacement, full race cams, an enlarged throttle body and etc. 
  The only short coming are the points in the distributor which can be changed to hall affect to improve reliability.
  In short the tools required to modify an analog computer are a lot less expensive and easier to change than the CPU systems. Oh I forgot to mention that the
electronics in the 914 are circa 1965 design practices, long before 8 bit micro
processors.
  I would be interested in knowing if any one else in this mailing has modified
the stock 914 electronics????

Date: Thu, 18 Jun 92 11:41:43 EDT
From: boubez@tastesgreat.rutgers.edu


Hi there. Sorry for the delayed response, but I'm on the Digest list, not
the "immediate" mailing list. For any responses, please include my email
address in the CC list. Anyway, I seem to remember having a similar
discussion on W2W not too long ago. I ended up using the Electromotive
system for several reasons.

1. It provides both injection and ignition.
2. Both VE and spark advance tables can easily be programmed through a PC.
   In addition, you can have several different tables stored on disk, each
   with a different setup and specs, and download whichever one is
   appropriate.
3. For a similar system it's much cheaper than the Haltech and certainly
   than the NOS system. And from conversations I've had with users of
   Haltech, they had lots of problems with it. Please remember, however,
   that this is only according to two Haltech users. It could be that they
   didn't know how to use it properly, that they had different expectations
   from it, etc.
4. I don't have the new version, but there's an article in the latest Turbo
   magazine that describes it. It has charting capabilities, and
   closed-loop tuning. This can make life A LOT easier, believe me.

The only problem I had with the TEC-II unit, as it's called, is that the
reference manual that comes with it is far from clear. Actually I don't
think that there was any direct correspondance between the manual and the
system that was shipped to me! I have to say, though, that they had lots of
on-line help in the program, but to me it's not the same as having a manual
in your hand. Take care.

*START OF STAN'S INFO*

OK, let's start off with some basic info on pulsed EFI. Think in terms of
what it is you want to do here. The goal is to have an engine which provides
a good trade between performance and drivability and economy, all of which is
of course nearly mutually exclusive 8{)

What do you have to work with, in terms of inputs? You have actual current
RPM, amount of airflow either in mass or volume (which is proportional to RPM),
manifold pressure (which can be indicative of impending changes in RPM), air
and engine temp, and that's about it. If you are talking later cars, you also
have the O2 or lambda sensor. If you are getting to do this from scratch, 
you can also add exhaust gas temp sensor, barometric pressure, fuel density,
and some other wild stuff.

And, in terms of things you can control to make any difference, you have
start time of EFI pulse relative to TDC, duration of pulse, and (potentially)
fuel presssure.

The question becomes: how to I modulate the outputs to match the inputs and
my expectation set to give me more performance? I think I've found some
answers. I've at least found an interesting way to look at the problem.

Some things to note: as the engine runs faster, pulsed EFI approximates CIS.
Think about it: as engine rotation time goes down, pulse width needed to 
deliver enough gas plus the relaxation time of the injector (time it takes to 
close) will *exceed* the rotational time of the engine. This, by the way, is
one of the reasons that injectors fire on every engine rotation, not just
on the intake rotation: its the only way to get enough gas in.

The above isn't quite true, but you have to keep another factor in mind: an
injector will flow so many lbs of gas per hour (normal measure of injector
size) if kept full open at a certain pressure. If pressure goes up, the
gas flow goes up (to a point) *and the spray pattern improves*; if pressure
goes down, gas flow goes down *and the spray becomes a stream, which doesn't
vaporize very well, and consequently burns very lean*. Moral: low pressure
isn't necessarily good. (But neither is high pressure).

To maximize efficiency, you want to burn gas at the 1:14.7 to air, which is the
stochiometric point. For maximum performance, you want to get this down around
1:12.4. Maximum economy is about 1:16 (max lean-out before detonation).

Now, the hard part: making changes to the engine, and how that impacts the
injection system. For CIS, if you can flow enough air, then to get more gas 
you have to have more fuel pressure. But if you just jack up the fuel pressure, 
you get over-rich idle since *you just moved the set-point up on a linear 
curve*. The good news is, you'll get more power on  top end. The bad news is 
you won't be able to use it because you'll have loaded your plugs up from
running over rich at idle. This is also true for D and L as well. 

So, you need something that will increase pressure under load. If manifold 
pressure approximates load, you can tie your fuel pressure to manifold pressure 
and win big here. The device that does this is called a rising rate regulator, 
and is very effective for CIS. Ditto for L-jet.

The suggestion is often made that you can change the inputs to the EFI brain
by inserting a resistor or some such in the path from the sensor. Any time
you do this, you are guaranteed to screw up some part of the RPM range. The
problem you are trying to solve is multi-dimensional and any linear input 
change may have the desired effect *in a certain range* but not through
the whole operating range.

Anyway, I can't talk specifically about what I've done on D-jet systems (yet)
because (a) it's still under test and talking would be premature, and (b) I
think I'm going to productize it. In a nutshell, though, here are the key
things I've noted:

1) if you increase fuel pressure, you must decrease pulse time at idle

2) if you don't increase fuel pressure, you starve at high RPM

3) the pulse width is controlled by the intersection of RPM and manifold
   pressure sense, with some input from temp sense.

4) manifold pressure sense is not adjustable from the factory

5) the throttle throat  has a theoretical flow rate that levels off at
   about 7000 RPM

6) a stock engine will explode at 7000 RPM if kept there very long

SInce changing the brain is illegal in the roadracing classes I live in,
I can say for the benefit of those not so constrained that I have a digital
reprogrammable replacement brain in the works that eats the current analog
inputs and controls the same old injectors. But, it's programmable against
about a dozen inputs with two maps, one for "normal" and one for "performance".
Should be connector-compatible with your current system, plus some optional
sensors if you want to add them. Not sure how much it would sell for, but 
hopefully less than HalTech!

On that topic as well: the HalTech system is OK, but very limited. There are 
only 16 RPM ranges in which you can diddle the settings. If you need more
granularity, you can't get it. They also have a *VERY* simple system that 
approximates air flow by RPM (just like D-jet!), or one that's more 
sophisticated and used manifold pressure too. The Electromotive TEC II is 
a very good system and is what we currently use on the GTU car. It's more 
flexible than HalTech. Electromotive is harder to deal with than HalTech, but 
their VAR network makes that a lot more bearable.

Anyway, I hope this makes sense, is useful, etc.

Stan

From: fwk@homxc.att.com
Date: Mon, 22 Jun 92 17:36 EDT
Original-From: homxc!fwk (F W Kerfoot, Iii +1 908 949 6348)
To: Stan Hanks <stan@math.uh.edu>
Cc: porschephiles@karazm.math.uh.edu
Subject: Re: EFI follow-up

>Subject: EFI follow-up
>Content-Type: text
>Content-Length: 11893
>
>Over the last week there has been some interesting traffic on this list
>regarding EFI for the 914. Since I've been doing a lot of work in my
>914 EFI and have both built an engine management system for a GT-1 racer
>and programmed two different systems for an IMSA GTU 911, I want to speak up
>here. 
>
>.....
>
>4) manifold pressure sense is not adjustable from the factory
>
The D-Jetronic manifold pressure sensor appears to not be adjustable,
but it really is, with a little tinkering. When you look at the unit,
the end without the connector has a protrusion filled with epoxy.
Under this epoxy is a set of adjustments. The sensor actually has
several different slopes of inductance (which is the way the brain box
reads vacuum) versus manifold vacuum, determined by two separate
pressure sensing diaphrams and mechanical stops. You can adjust the
mixture at high vacuum (e.g. at idle or light load) separately from
that at low vacuum (wide open throttle), and you can adjust the location
of the transition, all by mechanical adjustments.

To make a sensor adjustable, you need to remove the epoxy. You can remove
most of it from the outside with a small screwdriver, dental picks,
etc, depending on what you have handy. You will still have trouble
removing the threaded aluminum plug, however, due to epoxy in the threads.
Therefore, disassemble the unit (4 screws on early units, drill out
the rivets and replace with screws later on later cars), and screw the
aluminum plug into the case (away from the epoxy in the threads).
Then remove the last of the epoxy from the threads. Note that the
location of the aluminum plug is one of the adjustments (my recollection
is that this is the full throttle mixture), so note its location before
you remove it. Under the plug is a screw slot, which I believe is
overall mixture. The screw is surrounded by a hexagonal indentation,
which is the third adjustment (the transition point, I believe).
You will need a special tool (effectively a hollow allen wrench)
for the latter. I made one for myself. Once you look at how the sensor
is made, you should be able to figure out what adjustment does what.

I don't know if any of this is legal in a particular class, or of any
interest, but it offers more adjustment opportunities than you would
guess at first glance.

Also, on the subject of resistor changes, there is a lot of waveshaping,
etc. done in the brain box before the pulse width is established where
you could do much more than an overall mixture change, if you wanted
to play with analog circuits rather than computer tables.

Frank Kerfoot
fwk@homxc.att.com

(1) Brittania Rescue (along with the Automobile Association (AA),
Royal Automobile Association (RAC), National Breakdown and a myriad
more) are organisations which for a small yearly fee (about #25 or

or will bring your car and passengers anywhere in the UK if they
can't get the car running. Well worth the money for the peace of
mind. I don't know if the AAA or anyone else does a similar service
in the US.

Blaine -

I don't know anyone who has milled 944 rotors.  They wear well and get replaced
when they get too thin.  The wear limit is stamped on the hub.  I think it's
19 mm for most 944's.  I dunno about the folks you deal with, but I have the
impression that much of the rotor milling done in the world is a scam.  You
should not have to have your rotors milled unless there is a problem that has
been indentified and corrected (ie, not just to treat a symptom).  As far as I
know...

I think you want toe-in and not camber to correct shimmy.  2.5 negative degrees 
of camber is indeed a lot.

Kevin

Philip -

The first thing anyone checks is the idle stabilizer.  It can get clogged with
soot.  You should take it out and clean with with solvent, such as brake
cleaner.

The next thing I would check is for air and vacuum leaks.  The vacuum hose
routine is described (in US cars, at least) on a schematic pasted onto the wheel
well arch behind the windshield fluid bottle.  For air leaks, I would check the
whole induction system for loose clamps, and the manifold gasket and injector
o-rings.

Both my manifold gasket and a couple of the injectors were leaking a couple of
months back.  The symptoms were a weird idle/decel initially, then on one cold
day, I could not get the car to idle at all until really warmed up.  (I used my
right foot to idle at stop lights for the first five minutes.  Then all became
fine.)

You can test for air leaks with a cup of water.  Sprinkle the water at the
suspect, and listen for a change in the engine idle as well as a sucking noise
as it is drawn in.  You'll also see the water disappear where there's a leak. 
The same thing can be done with a bottle of brake cleaner.  You'll also smell it
in the exhaust as it combusts.  Do no use a freon-containing liquid if you value
your lungs!

I replaced the gasket and all injectors' o-rings under the tutelage of my
mechanic.  No big deal, I learned, except that pressing the plastic retainer
caps onto the injectors is a real pain.  The parts are not expensive.

Kevin

John, $8500 sounds about right for this car.  These cars do not seem
to hold their value as well as the 944 series. (Just not as sexy...)

The 924S has the 944 8-valve engine, a slimmer body style, narrower
tires and wheels, the old-style (pre-1985/2) interior, and a
marginally higher top speed.

Things to watch out for:

1) Water Pump
2) Oil Cooler leaking into Cooling System
3) Power Steering
4) Timing Belt Replacements

>
>I think you want toe-in and not camber to correct shimmy.  2.5 negative degrees 
>of camber is indeed a lot.
>

In fact, the original post referred to 2.5 degrees of caster (not camber or
toe). 2.5 degrees of toe-in would also be a lot. Caster is best viewed
as the fore-aft inclination of the axis about which the wheel assembly
rotates as you turn the steering wheel.

Frank

Received: from KGNVMY by vnet.ibm.com (IBM VM SMTP V2R2) with BSMTP id 1438;
   Wed, 24 Jun 92 08:50:47 EDT
Date: Wed, 24 Jun 92 08:42:43 EDT
From: "George Beuselinck" <gb@vnet.ibm.com>
To: porschephiles@karazm.math.uh.edu
Subject: Milling 944 Rotors

Turning brake rotors may be acceptable on a street-only car, but, for
me, since I do track the car, I chose to put new rotors on when my
originals were noticeably worn.  The cost was not that significant
and I took the opportunity to change the wheel bearings at the same
time.  I feel much more confident about my brakes knowing that I have
full thickness rotors to dissipate the heat I generate at the end of
a long straight.

Although most people do not take their Porsches out into the snow and
salt, for the cars that they drive in the winter, rotors tend to rot
from the inside out (especially on vented rotors) and turning them
merely exposes metal that is closer to the rust.

BTW, the +2.5 degrees mentioned in Upfixin for the 944 vibration problem
is for caster, not camber.  There's a big difference!
       ------

                              24 Jun 1992 08:50:49
George Beuselinck

It's a 924S.  It has the 944 engine, but the 924 body.
Because it lacks the wheel bulges (in my opinion, the
bulgeless design is much cleaner and more elegant), it
uses narrower tires, but has better areodynamics and a
higher top speed.  Although the suspension layout is
exactly the same as the 944, it uses stamped steel
control arms on the front instead of cast alloy.  Thus
it may not last as long on the track, but then again
it may.  Because of 944 snobbery, you can get an
extremely good deal on a 924S, and (again my opinion)
have even more fun because you're not worried about
losing a $30,000 Porsche!  _MS

Cc: porschephiles <porschephiles@karazm.math.uh.edu>
Subject: Re: Ignition distributor vacuum
Message-Id: <42920627172824/0004056890NA1EM@mcimail.com>

John -

Any car with vacuum (dynamic) advance also has a fixed advance which is
adjustable.  I would check it.  This is usually very simple: pull the vacumm
hose off the distributor and clamp it, then use a timing light to set the
advance.

If merely changing your cap and rotor brought this one, you know where to look! 
You should be able to get Bosch part numbers off both old and new and compare
them.  Any Bosch servive centre should be able to get you the right part numbers
from their fiche set for a Euro car.

Your problem could also be due to the bob weights getting hung up in the
distributor.

Make sure you put the rotor on correctly.  Generally, they are keyed and cannot
be put on incorrectly.  (On the other hand, my 944S rotor (same as US 928S
except for longer bolts and spacers) is not keyed.  You have to remember how it
came off.  If you put it on wrong, the engine would plain not run, rather than
lose power as your describe.)

Happy hunting!

Kevin

1.      I get between 4 and 24 mpg in my 1980 sc coupe.  4 when

        it does it under load.  My knowledge of FI problems is limited,

		Peter Morgan
		Published by Foulis/Haynes, 1990
		ISBN 0-85429-764-2
		Library of Congress catalogue card number 90-81744

	On another tack, I can highly recommend (even though I haven't
finished reading it!):

		"The PORSCHE Driving Book" (2nd edition)
		Martin Beck-Burridge & John Lyon
		Published by Porsche Cars Great Britain Limited, 1990
		ISBN 0-9513737-1-4

which is available from:

		Porsche Cars Great Britain Limited
		Bath Road
		Calcot
		Reading
		Berkshire
		England
		RG3 7SE

and cost 19.95 Pounds Sterling when I bought my copy from a local dealer.
This is the book which PCGB give away free with every new Porsche, and
deals with advanced driving techniques like advanced observation and the
overtaking triangle, as well as less useful things like heel & toe.
Interestingly it recommends that you always double declutch (something I
have been practising in hire cars:-) ). There are also separate sections
on how to drive the 911/944/928/turbo.


	The local Porsche Club GB had a street concours on Sunday and I
managed to get a lift in the local dealer's 968 demonstrator - they
wouldn't let anyone drive it because they had had a 911 totalled recently
at a recent event :-( The salesman who was driving wouldn't go over 120mph
:-( :-( but he did give me a demonstration of the 3 second 60-0 time which
was impressive. A desireable car, but I really did feel that it was a 944
S3 rather than a totally new car.

	I also got a copy of the Performance Products catalogue which a
local company is importing. How do these people rate for price and quality?
